                                                 <h3>
                                                  Article about Melvyn Bragg's Radical Lives: Rights of Man – Thomas Paine:
                                                 </h3>
                                                </div>
                                               </div>
                                               <div class="stacks_out" id="stacks_out_14069_page143">
                                                <div class="stacks_in com_rapidweavercentral_stacks_theshadow_stack" id="stacks_in_14069_page143">
                                                 <!-- DesignThing by RapidWeaver Central. Visit http://www.quantumweaving.com for more information. -->
                                                 <div class="shadowthing dash 0 shadow-styledefaultstacks_in_14069_page143 static-bg3stacks_in_14069_page143 rollover-bgstacks_in_14069_page143" id="designthingstacks_in_14069_page143">
                                                  <div class="designlink" id="designlinkstacks_in_14069_page143">
                                                   <!--No link to display-->
                                                  </div>
                                                  <div class="" id="theSlice">
                                                   <div class="stacks_out" id="stacks_out_13876_page143">
                                                    <div class="stacks_in text_stack" id="stacks_in_13876_page143">
                                                     <span id="stacks_in_13877_page143">
                                                      <a href="http://www.telegraph.co.uk/culture/11021445/Melvyn-Bragg-Thomas-Paine-was-a-great-Englishman.html" rel="external">
                                                       Melvyn Bragg: 'Thomas Paine was a great Englishman'
                                                      </a>
                                                      (August 9, 2014, The Telegraph)
                                                      <br/>
                                                      <blockquote>
                                                       (…) His first book, Common Sense, written in 1775, was taken by the American leaders of what became their Revolution – especially George Washington – as a summons that it was their duty to throw off the colonial yoke.
                                                       <br/>
                                                       <br/>
                                                       His next publication, The Rights of Man (1791), had, like Common Sense, startling success, and also attacked the English constitution. It sold more copies than the Bible and it turned the British government against him.
                                                       <br/>
                                                       <br/>
                                                       In his third book, The Age of Reason he dismantled the Bible stories with a ferocity of reason which again infused the imagination of an immense audience.
                                                       <br/>
                                                       <br/>
                                                       (…)
                                                       <br/>
                                                       <br/>
                                                       He was far ahead of his time. “When it shall be said in any country in the world, my poor are happy… my jails are empty of prisoners, my streets of beggars; the aged are not in want; the taxes are not oppressive… then may that country boast its constitution and its government.”
                                                       <br/>
                                                       <br/>
                                                       But yet again he attacked those who had elevated him. He objected to all capital punishment including the execution of the King and Queen of France. The French threw him out of Parliament and into prison, where he just escaped the guillotine.
                                                       <br/>
                                                       <br/>
                                                       (…)
                                                       <br/>
                                                       <br/>
                                                       He died in America in 1809, having been given a small pension by a few of his friends. Even fewer turned up – six – at his funeral.
                                                      </blockquote>
                                                      <br/>
                                                      <strong>
                                                       From the Comments:
                                                      </strong>
                                                      <br/>
                                                      <blockquote>
                                                       "An illiterate mechanic, when mistaking some disturbance of his nerves for a miraculous call proceeds alone to convert a tribe of savages, whose language he can have no natural means of acquiring, may have been misled by impulses very different from those of high self opinion ; but the illiterate perpetrator of " the Age of Reason," must have had his very conscience stupefied by the habitual intoxication of presumptuous arrogance, , and his common sense over-clouded by the vapours of his head"
                                                       <br/>
                                                       <br/>
                                                       <a href="http://www.archive.org/stream/cu31924105501526/cu31924105501526_djvu.txt" rel="external">
                                                        Coleridge on Paine and his ilk
                                                       </a>
                                                       .
                                                      </blockquote>
                                                     </span>
                                                    </div>
                                                   </div>
                                                  </div>

                                        <div class="stacks_in text_stack" id="stacks_in_13169_page88">
                                         Here is the proof.
                                         <br/>
                                         <br/>
                                         Below is the wording on the plaque in the Oxford University Museum of Natural History on the very spot where Sir Oliver Lodge sent the first radio signal on August 14, 1894 at the Oxford meeting of The British Association.
                                         <br/>
                                        </div>
                                       </div>
                                       <div class="stacks_out" id="stacks_out_13203_page88">
                                        <div class="stacks_in com_joeworkman_stacks_fancy-image_caption_stack" id="stacks_in_13203_page88">

                                          <img alt="Stacks Image 13204" class="imageStyle" height="328" src="index_files/stacks_image_13204.jpg" width="700"/>
                                         </div>
                                         <div id="caption_stacks_in_13203_page88">
                                          <em>
                                           Plaque in the Museum Lecture Theatre, Oxford University Museum of Natural History, Parks Road, Oxford
                                          </em>
                                         </div>
                                        </div>
                                       </div>
                                       <div class="stacks_out" id="stacks_out_13195_page88">
                                        <div class="stacks_in text_stack" id="stacks_in_13195_page88">
                                         The text of the plaque reads:
                                        </div>
                                       </div>
                                       <div class="stacks_out" id="stacks_out_13184_page88">
                                        <div class="stacks_in com_rapidweavercentral_stacks_theshadow_stack" id="stacks_in_13184_page88">
                                         <!-- DesignThing by RapidWeaver Central. Visit http://www.quantumweaving.com for more information. -->
                                         <div class="shadowthing dash 0 shadow-styledefaultstacks_in_13184_page88 static-bg3stacks_in_13184_page88 rollover-bgstacks_in_13184_page88" id="designthingstacks_in_13184_page88">
                                          <div class="designlink" id="designlinkstacks_in_13184_page88">
                                           <!--No link to display-->
                                          </div>
                                          <div class="" id="theSlice">
                                           <div class="stacks_out" id="stacks_out_13193_page88">
                                            <div class="stacks_in text_stack" id="stacks_in_13193_page88">
                                             <span id="stacks_in_13194_page88">
                                              <div align="center">
                                               Oliver Lodge Centenary of Radio Transmission
                                               <br/>
                                               <br/>
                                               This plaque commemorates the centenary of the first public demonstration of wireless telegraphy, the precursor of modern radio. In this lecture theatre on 14 August 1894 at the Oxford meeting of The British Association, Sir Oliver Lodge, F.R.S., demonstrated the reception of a morse code signal transmitted from the old Clarendon Laboratory, some sixty metres away.
                                               <br/>
                                               <br/>
                                               Sharp Laboratories of Europe generously funded this plaque which was unveiled by Sir Michael Atiyah, O.M., President of The Royal Society.
                                              </div>
                                             </span>
                                            </div>
                                           </div>
                                          </div>
                                         </div>

                                     <table class="comments" width="100%">
                                      <tr>
                                       <td>
                                        <p>
                                         <strong>
                                          The Scientific Discovery of the Spiritual Part of the Universe
                                         </strong>
                                         <br/>
                                         <br/>
                                         In 1933, the inventor of radio,
                                         <a href="articles/toc/articles-lodge.html" rel="self" title="Sir Oliver Lodge (June 12, 1851 – August 22, 1940)">
                                          Sir Oliver Lodge
                                         </a>
                                         (1851 - 1940), gave a cosmological location for the spiritual part of the universe with his scientific paper
                                         <a href="articles/scientists/lodge/mofe.html" rel="self" title="The Mode of Future Existence - by Sir Oliver Lodge (1933)">
                                          The Mode of Future Existence
                                         </a>
                                         . This vital scientific discovery has been suppressed and kept from the people.
                                         <br/>
                                         <br/>
                                         In 1894, Sir Oliver Lodge sent the first radio signal. However, twenty years before this, in 1874,
                                         <a href="articles/toc/articles-crookes.html" rel="self" title="Sir William Crookes (June 17, 1832 – April 4, 1919)">
                                          Sir William Crookes
                                         </a>
                                         (1832 - 1919) had discovered the spiritual part of the universe by carrying out repeatable experiments under laboratory conditions, and had published the results in The Quarterly Journal of Science. Crookes was later made the president of The Royal Society and awarded The Order of Merit.
                                         <br/>
                                         <br/>
                                         Crookes' experiments proved that it was scientifically possible for, for example, Jesus to appear in front of his disciples in his "spiritual" body, proving to them that he had survived the death of his physical body.
                                        </p>
                                        <br/>
                                        <br/>
                                       </td>
                                      </tr>
                                     </table>
